B000RGH4FQ|5.0|The pairing of Irene Papas and Vangelis Papathanassiou is an experience for the ears. Papas is an accomplished actress with a very strong voice and that inexplicable presence most of us don't have. She has acted on stage and in films, as well as singing on numerous records over the years, and for fans of progressive rock, \\"Prog,\\" if you will, she's best known for her vocal performance on Aphrodite's Child's \\"666\\" record. Uptight people malign it for a supposed satanic reference, but the text, though contemporary, is taken entirely from the Bible's Book Of Revelation. It gripes me when people call it \\"Revelations,\\" there is no \\"s.\\" It's the Revelation to John, on the isle of Patmos, the Revelation, not Revelations. In her featured piece on that record, Papas chants, groans, whispers, and sings \\"I was, I am to come, I was, I am!\\" for several minutes, edited down from an original session of a good half-hour, just her voice depicting the throes of hysteria and sexual arousal accompanied by some percussion instruments. It was very controversial at the time, declared obscene in some European countries which are more restrictive when regulating performance art. No governing body should ever control this area; the free market should dictate if it succeeds or fails. If it's good and it sells, fine. If it's corrupt and sells, it would be emblematic of a society gone wrong. You then address human behavior, but don't restrict something because certain powerful busybodies don't like it. If it's good, people will like it.<br /><br />In \\"Odes,\\" Irene Papas does a more generally appealing performance, \\"normal\\" singing as it were, and where I don't speak a word of the Greek language, I enjoy listening to this. \\"40 Braves\\" opens this set, bold, theatrical, and operatic in nature, it sets the mood. One feels like they are in a theater balcony watching some inscrutable, but unmissable stage play.<br /><br />\\"Little Orange Tree\\" is more of the same; layered, orchestrated music with Papas' voice being the perfect vehicle for this material.<br /><br />Vangelis Papathanassiou wrote and performed all the music on this release, and the first all instrumental piece, called \\"Fire Dance,\\" follows. It sounds like some odd soundtrack to some unknown film, fusing progressive and traditional sounds in a rambling performance with different moods and tempos; great headphone music.<br /><br />\\"The Kolokotronei\\" follows, an a capella vocal by Papas, and again, I don't understand a single word of this. But Greek is a nice sounding language, and her delivery is soothing; she's got a very beautiful voice.<br /><br />\\"The River\\" has this otherworldly vibe to it, very serene, an ethereal picture in sound, the arrangement reminds me of something Mike Oldfield would come up with.<br /><br />\\"The Roots\\" is pure New Age, the type of music NPR used to play late on Sunday nights, definitely music to space out to. Pink Floyd cornered that market, but selections like this prove they don't occupy that area alone. It's almost Oriental in its flavor, but as it goes on, it gets more and more Floydian in nature.<br /><br />\\"Lamento\\" is an eerie-sounding dirge, a very dark and sinister mood; it could easily be the soundtrack to a scary piece of film, with its droning keyboards and heavily reverberated vocals, it's almost post apocalyptic. Very somber.<br /><br />\\"Menoussis\\" sounds Asian. it just has that kind of vibe to it. I know it's Greek, but it does sound like it could be in a pleasant Japanese movie. Most New Age music sounds like it could be soundtrack music, but listening to this, I think of Oriental movies, not a bad thing, just a consequence, intentional or not.<br /><br />Bottom line, \\"Odes\\" is a great piece of world music, often soothing, sometimes disturbing, but always of the highest caliber.the perfect vehicle for this material. 1591843790|4.0|This March marks the 8th year I have been teaching entrepreneurship as a subject. In every group of 35 students I teach I come across a handful on fire. The wild, out of control, let me change the world fire, as well as the slow burning, smoldering, I will get through everything fire.  All I have to do is to make them see the end goal and they are generally able to figure the rest out.<br /><br />Over the years in the list of questions that students ask as I help them get their ideas closer to commercial reality the most common, the most troublesome and the most awkward question is:<br /><br />How do I sell? How do I convince people to say yes? How do I persuade them to give me a chance and a shot?<br /><br />While you can always direct them to books on marketing, business development and sales, to my knowledge no one ever wrote a book that gave you a roadmap to do all three. Typical text on marketing cover dimensions around marketing decisions (the C's and P's) or industrial or retail branding, business development talk about the art of deal making, closing and negotiating and tomes on selling talk about prospecting and lead generation. But there is hardly one book that you can point a student or a dreamer to and say read it and at least you will have a place to start.<br /><br />For example from Chapter 5 on How to Launch, pick a neat set of tools including<br /><br />a) Tell a Story.  Everyone wants to buy or share your dream, not a number.<br />b) Present the big choice first. Then the small one (the one you really want)<br />c) Reduce the number of choices. (Make it simple - thought Guy isn't sure if it works the other way round also).<br />d) Sell the salient point.<br /><br />I have always liked Guy's way of breaking the Art of the Start down to a science and his admission that it is not science. He first did it with his Art of the Start speech (the California TIECON edition). The speech is now a regular feature on day two of my classes and the framework he presents is what I use with my students to put flesh around the bones of their ideas.  For instance in the part where we help students move their concept to the real world we ask them a few qualifying questions.<br /><br />a) Why do they care about their idea and can they describe it in such a fashion that their audience can feel their passion and energy?<br />b) Why do their customers care about this product and how would they describe it?<br />c) How do they create meaning with their work and is it (or would it) be really a worthwhile use of their time, energy and life?<br /><br />As students stumble around inside themselves trying to find the right answers you can immediately tell who has been on fire and dreaming for how long.  For the dreamers and the fire people have it down pat. They know the answers before the question is even asked. For others the questions serve as a sifter that filters dreams from noise, meaning from mindless chatter.<br /><br />The questions above originated from the Art of the Start. And seeing them at work really give you a lot of respect for Guy's list of processes and checklists. Which is one way of looking at Enchantment - It's a book of checklists for dreamers. Not touchy feely, hand waving stuff but things that you can use tonight and see if Guy's trial by fire approach works for you.  Individuals like me like me who are founders and geeks in search of an algorithm to persuade and influence people so that we can go out and change the world by converting others to our cause.<br /><br />Not sell but convert; not convince but enchant.nd the bones of their ideas. 044656740X|3.0|Submitted by ALEX PRONOVE IS535<br />Book Review of Rich Dad, Poor Dad April 16, 2008<br /><br />More than anything else--Rich Dad, Poor Dad--is an instructional book on Financial Literacy.<br /><br />Its lessons are presented as a captivating story--a disarming way to introduce several concepts--some of which upset established notions of wealth, its accumulation and its related aspects.<br /><br />The dichotomy of beliefs between the two fathers surfaces early in the story. The differences between their beliefs are emphasized to make the point unmistakably clear. The subjects of their difference are not trivial. These are education, work, and, in a general manner, the approach to life.<br />The remainder of the book expounds on this theme.<br /><br />Did this book make an impression on me? Yes, it did. I agree, for example, with the Rich Dad's definition of a Liability. Since reading the book, I view home ownership as the homeowners' voluntary assumption of a liability--whether they realize it or not. The recent run-up in prices from 2000 to early-2006 persuaded homeowners to treat their homes as piggybanks. No longer! Real estate appreciation barely beats inflation in the long run. Historical data supports that.<br />I enjoyed the discussion on financial statements and how they differ between an individual and a bank. Equally enlightening was my introduction to the CashFlow Quadrant.<br /><br />According to Kiyosaki, financial literacy consists of five principles.<br />1. How to make money<br />2. How to protect your money<br />3. How to budget your money<br />4. How to leverage your money<br />5. How to continuously  improve your financial IQ<br /><br />I strongly agree with his contention that our educational system is making a serious mistake by ignoring financial literacy in its curriculum. Many people would benefit from knowing those five principles, the quadrant, and even the contrarian definitions of assets, liabilities, and so forth.<br />In my opinion, Rich Dad, Poor Dad is worth reading. I don't think it has a lot of fluff. It introduces a lot of \\"heretical\\" concepts but the best thing it may do for the reader is it may open his/her mind.es a lot of \\"heretical\\" concepts but the best thing it may do for the reader is it may open his/her mind.

1439163146|3.0|In terms of fronts in the ever shifting culture wars, actor/ intellectual -- and now author -- James Franco's short story collection, Palo Alto should be pretty weak tea.  That hasn't stopped critics from taking up sides declaring this debut alternatively \\"delectable\\" and \\"too true\\" (the pro-team) or featuring characters described as \\"mere sketches\\" and one who \\"felt the urge to grade it.  And not highly\\" (the anti).    I'm retreating from the field.  Perhaps I'm copping out (or just being honest?) by admitting that I can't separate this actor from his collection.  Let me just say that these stories are, at best, uneven.  Some declare as such it wouldn't have been published but for Franco's fame, but examining debuts(where uneven is more the rule than the exception), this is plainly not the case.  Undeniably, other don't receive such close attention, either from those who want to denigrate or those heaping on profligate praise (Perhaps they fantasize about invitations to posh parties?  Optioned novel?)    Franco studied in several top writing programs.  And it shows.  His eye for detail is excellent.  He crafts fine, sparse sentences.  This, however, doesn't make up for these stories emotional monotony; one after another they recount angst ridden teenagers suffering the tragedy of suburban life.  In and of itself, this isn't bad - teen angst is rich soil to plow - but here it's been farmed to dust.  Characters become undifferentiated, indeed, interchangeable.      Had Franco included a sampling of the best of this collection with other differently themed works - surely other people live in this zip code with richer emotional contours - Palo Alto could well have been excellent.  In \\"American History\\" a boy assigned to defend slavery in a high school debate foolishly engages in over heated rhetoric seeking to impress a girl, and in so doing reveals much of the racial strife that still lurks below the surface.  And angst.  \\"Lockheed\\" offers a meditation on growing up and what it means to be good at something.  And, of course, angst.    Even as Franco seeks to increase the energy of these stories with more and more helpings of bloody violence, these too become over used, becoming indistinguishable as his characters.  While there are plainly signs of potential here, one can only hope any future projects will include greater variety and depth.

1401309666|4.0|Chris Anderson, the editor-in-chief of \\"Wired\\" magazine wrote The Long Tail after posting many articles and blogs regarding this theory.  The Long Tail that Anderson speaks of expands upon the tail end of an economics curve that never seems to end.  He has done research that shows the amount of sales in the niche and obscure market is what really keeps businesses alive.   Many of his examples are related to the entertainment industry: books, television, movies and music.  These types of products support the Long Tail theory.  He dives really deep into each subset of the entertainment industry and all of their marketing providers.  The rise of I-Tunes has had an enormous impact on the music industry and the number of \\"records\\" the number one hit sells.  He notes that there will always be \\"hits\\" but the Internet has truly taken over the providers of books, DVDs, music, and much more.     Anderson brings up the Pareto Rule or 80/20 rule, on more than one occasion.  One example of how to explain the Pareto Rule is that 80% of sales come from 20% of the products.   His theory is that the Pareto rule no longer exists.   This is because sales still occur in many different products.  Some may not be as much as 80%, but they are still contributing to the sales. He has some statistics to support this theory, but I still believe that it is a solid rule to follow when small businesses are doing strategic planning.   With the number of choices we have today, consumers expect to be provided an unlimited number of choices when making a purchase.  That is why people shop at Amazon.com rather than going to a neighborhood bookstore.  Really, at Amazon.com, the choices are almost endless.    At the conclusion, Anderson even gives tips of how to create a \\"Long Tail business.\\" His main two tips are: \\"1. Make Everything Available, and 2. Help me find it.\\" (pg 217) Anderson makes it appear as though if you don't have a long tail business, then you may not survive.  I agree with that in some sense.  As time goes on, the internet is going to be the main source for shopping and the sites with the most choices and easiest navigation will be the survivors.   I found this book to be extremely interesting from both a marketing and economics perspective.  The research, data presented and analysis performed was supporting of all Anderson's thoughts and theories.  However, I did find it to be quite repetitive and the points could have been made in half the length.
B004SBS8LA|5.0|I enjoyed the original and this one was no different. All of the Angry Birds games are addictive, challenging, and fun. I really like the tropical theme. I really don't understand the negative reviews on here. How could a free and awesome app be bad?

B004ISVH7U|5.0|If you were alive and aware in 1969, you know it was anything but a time of confidences. I remember being 15 and getting tear-gassed at the Washington Monument in the middle of an angry war protest on the Fourth of July among 250,000 people ... and I was just there to see Bob Hope and the Beach Boys. Robert Kennedy and Martin Luther King were assassinated the year before. Cities burned down during the riots afterward. The daily news was a parade of body count numbers from Vietnam. The country was torn between Nixon supporters, anti-war protestors, hippies, Black Panther radicals, John Birch conservatives, poverty, racism, and migrant and other abused workers struggling for decent working conditions through collective bargaining (oops, bye bye). But in total counterpoint to the chaos came a sound as pure and serene and ... confident as humanly possible. Two friends who had been singing together since they were 11 year-old pups were just now hitting their peak with \\"Bridge Over Troubled Water;\\" an album that captured lyrical, vocal and engineering mastery beyond measure.<br /><br />There is no fill on the album. Nothing mediocre. It launches you into the stratosphere on the opening title cut and never lets up. It's one sustained mood of mixed emotions brilliantly recorded after another. No mere \\"Greatest Hits\\" album by the same duo could ever match the level of sustained inspiration woven here. Paul Simon and Art Garfunkel knew it. They split up after this. How could it ever by topped? Well, there are still some surprises left for us in the seen-and-heard-it-all 2011, and this 40th anniversary edition comes not only with a remastered version of the album, but a Simon and Garfunkel CBS television special that originally aired in 1969, PLUS a new documentary interviewing the key players on the making the of the album. And every moment is revelation.<br /><br />Simon and Garfunkel had four of the top five chart positions at the time and were so popular that a one-hour network special on CBS gave them carte blanche to do whatever they wanted. So they did a wandering meditative tone poem of moving images on America featuring John F. Kennedy, Martin Luther King and Robert Kennedy's funeral train while \\"Bridge Over Troubled Water\\" played over. That featured young couples in love contrasted with violent and fiery war images from Vietnam while \\"Scarborough Faire\\" played. That featured widow Coretta King talking about poverty over disturbing images of diseased and starving children. And they ended (big sigh of relief from the network), with a brief on stage concert. Naturally, millions of shocked viewers choked on their nightcap cocktails and tumbled out of their easy chairs to switch the channel over to the Peggy Fleming Ice Skating special on ABC. When director Charles Grodin (yes, THAT Charles Grodin), screened the Coretta King voiceover poverty section to the network brass, they asked him if he could adjust the audio on it. \\"How do you want it?\\" he asked. \\"Inaudible,\\" they replied.<br /><br />The original sponsor dropped out, but Alberto VO5 stepped in (hey, there was a lot of hair on young viewers in 1969), and the show aired as produced. Try watching it in the context of 1969, or even prime time network television TODAY, and you will gasp at what they got away with. And if you can watch Robert Kennedy's funeral train pass through the countryside by waving mourners as \\"Bridge Over Troubled Water\\" reaches its crescendo, and without crying, you need to check the dose level of your anti-depressants. You just might be catatonic.<br /><br />Take a deep breath after the television special, thinking you've struck unearthed gold never seen since 1969, but here comes a fantastic new documentary about the making of the album (and the special), and nirvana kicks in. If you care about music at all, or how it is created or inspired, or recorded, you will be entranced. Paul Simon reveals the gospel music he was listening to when the inspiration struck for \\"Bridge,\\" which he readily acknowledges is beyond any rational explanation. Art Garfunkel convinces him to add the third verse taking it even higher. Their genius engineer, Roy Halee, master of finding the perfect echo, records the \\"li li li\\" chorus of \\"The Boxer\\" in a stone church chapel to get the right haunting tones. He records the drum crescendos for \\"Bridge\\" outside the elevators at CBS to the shock and awe of departing passengers. Garfunkel and Simon playfully slap their hands on their denim-covered knees in a hotel room, roll the Sony recorder, create a one-minute loop, and inadvertently come up with the entire rhythm backing for \\"Cecilia.\\" And on and on.<br /><br />I don't know about you, but I always get thrills from hearing artists describe their moments of inspiration. That's my crack addiction. The joy of invention, of innovation, of seeking that perfect sound infuses everything they did or discuss here. And you share that joy of discovery with them. Unless of course, your lithium dosage won't let you.<br /><br />Troubled outer times call for a stillness of inner peace. Simon and Garfunkel somehow sensed that delicate balance in 1969 and distilled a sound for the ages with this masterpiece. Witness the creation of that same masterpiece 40 years later to understand how the silences within these sounds are needed more than ever.dily acknowledges is beyond any rational explanation. 0671646788|5.0|How highly do I think of this book? Put it this way... had I have read this book in the first place, I might have never bothered putting up my practicalgoalachieving blog (well, OK... I might have still put it up ;-))! Seriously... if I had to write a self-help book, this would probably be it.<br /><br />I say \\"probably\\" because there are some \\"minor\\" problems I have with it. So, I guess \\"5 stars\\" is not quite correct. How about \\"4.5 stars\\" or 4.7 stars?<br /><br />But, anyway, about the book: The funny thing is, as I was going through the intro and the first chapter, the material in this book really didn't \\"jump\\" out at me as anything special. It was pretty much about how to think bigger, how to believe in yourself, goals and solid beliefs vs. wishes, etc.... Not bad advice, but being someone who is an avid reader of self-help and personal development books, this was pretty much things I could have read... well... anywhere. Even on some of those over-priced e-books on Clickbank (thank goodness for their 60 day refund policy).<br /><br />Also (and this was one of the very, very few problems I had with the book), as I was reading the first chapter, I kinda got the impression that much of the premise of the book was more or less a rehash of Law of Attraction, especially in one of the examples, where the author gave an example of a guy who asked for a $3,500.00 raise and got it just like that (although I would suspect there was more to the story. Then again, it's not my story).<br /><br />There are other minor problems I have with it, such as the section on fear. The author was talking about the types of fears and the \\"prescribed\\" action to take (according to him). I kinda had to smirk on one of those examples, when he talked about \\"fear of tornado\\" and the action to take: switch your attention to something totally different. Go to the yard and pull out weeds.... Yeah, OK. So, like what if there really WAS a tornado? Will pulling out weeds reverse the course of the tornado? In the beginning of the chapter, the author says \\"most fear today is psychological\\". Yeah. MOST. It's the ones that are not (like the tornado that really will come and won't disappear just because you go to the garden and pull out weeds) that you should really be concerned about.<br /><br />Also, and this is not so much this book as much as almost all (if not pretty much all) of the books I read, but the author does repeat himself in some areas, such as taking action on fear. But at least he does it in such a way that's not to, well... annoying (for lack of a better word).<br /><br />So, why the 5 stars (or the 4.5 stars, but Amazon can't do 4.5)? Simply put, aside from the minor problems, this book truly is dead on on alot of the points. Reading this book is, in many ways like reading [[ASIN:1439167346 How To Win Friends and Influence People]],[[ASIN:1416571035 The 28 Laws of Attraction: Stop Chasing Success and Let It Chase You]], [[ASIN:0060594896 The Success Principles(TM): How to Get from Where You Are to Where You Want to Be]], [[ASIN:1605094110 Goals!: How to Get Everything You Want -- Faster Than You Ever Thought Possible]] and, yes... even [[ASIN:1582701709 The Secret]] all together (heck... I could have probably saved myself the trouble of reading those books and purchased THIS instead!), as so many of the elements of those books are found in this. Yet, for some reason, this book puts the material in a way that really relates to me personally and even though I'm sure it's not the case, but reading this book is almost like coming across the material for the first time.<br /><br />Perhaps the thing that most resonates with me and something that many of the self-help books totally misunderstand is the subject of persistence, which the authors pretty much covers flawlessly. Many of the self-help books I've read tells you things such as \\"you've got to keep trying\\", \\"don't ever give up\\" or my personal favorite: \\"most people quit on the one yard line\\". Theauthor here talks about persistence, but talks about it in relation to something else he calls EXPERIMENTATION. In my practicalgoalachieving blog, I talk about this, too but call it something else, but it's pretty much similar. Basically, what the author says here is that it doesn't matter how \\"persistent\\" you are. If you're using bad strategies and techniques and you don't try different methods, you will still fail, and the author reinforces this point when he talked about an aspiring writer who was persistent in that he was a very diligent worker, but the techniques and he was using in his writing (and ultimately the manuscripts he was submitting) was terrible.<br /><br />Long story short: Yes, you may be at the one yard line. But if you use bad strategies and techniques, then your direction is wrong and you're at the one yard line facing the WRONG ENDZONE! Now, I know that in the subject of \\"persistence\\", many gurus will tell you one of the reasons why people are stuck where they are is because they don't persist. However, I personally feel that there are at least some who DO persist, and where persistence is NOT the problem, but they are stuck for other reasons (such as not experimenting enough).<br /><br />So that's one thing I absolutely LOVE about this book. The other thing is how this book truly focuses on improving one's self. Not just the inner, but the outer appearance as well (this is one part in my life that I have to work on). Yeah, the book [[ASIN:1416571035 The 28 Laws of Attraction: Stop Chasing Success and Let It Chase You]] also covers it, but with this book, it's not as extreme. That's just a personal preference for me. I feel outward appearance is important (just ask almost any person conducting a job interview), but overdoing it is not necessary. For developing your inner self, the author talks about the importance of listening, how to be sincerely interested in helping other people and how to treat people. Alot of the things he talks about is in [[ASIN:1439167346 How To Win Friends and Influence People]], and in that respect I personally feel this is an excellent complement to Dale Carnegie's book.<br /><br />Speaking of how to treat people, I really loved two of the examples he uses: one involves how a manager handled a situation where an employee came to work drunk, and the other one was how a boss handled terminating (yes, TERMINATING) an employee. Not for bad behavior or anything, but for sub-par work. I won't give away the story here, but let me tell you this: if more bosses and managers handled situations the way the two bosses in these stories handled their respective situations, there would be much, much fewer turnovers and you would have much fewer employee complaints. This is one of the few books that discusses this \\"touchy\\" subject.<br /><br />In summary, and as a side note, I'd like to say this: There are so many books that talk about Law of Attraction, and I've even read some of these books (see my Amazon review page and practicalgoalachieving blog for more), and the author even kinda goes into some of the Law of Attraction stuff, such as affirmations (the author uses the example of \\"creating your own commercial\\"), mission statements and vision. But to me, THIS is what the Law of Attraction is: Taking care of yourself on the outside (but not to the extent of overkill... unless you want to) and (to me, at least) even more important: being a fantastic person on the inside. One that treats people and respect others well. To be wonderful to yourself so you could be even more wonderful to others. When you do those things, people will be attracted to you. They will hang around you and if you're a business person, they will be inclined to bring you business. THAT, to me, is the REAL Law of Attraction and to me the essence of this book.it in relation to something else he calls EXPERIMENTATION. B003Y5H50G|3.0|Sometimes movies have concepts so grand and large-scale that potential viewers can't help but imagine to themselves in advance what they would do with such technology, powers, knowledge, etc.  Imagine if you had the ability to travel through time.  What would you do?  When would you go?  Our imaginations are sparked, and thus, movies about time travel are often disappointing, because their reach never achieves what we'd imagine ourselves doing.    LIMITLESS suffers from this as well.  Bradley Cooper plays an author with a serious case of writer's block.  He's smart, but messy and disorganized.  He's handsome but can't be bothered to take care of his appearance.  And he can't get going on the book he's supposed to write.  His girlfriend (Abbie Cornish) is finally tired of him and leaves.  But hope arrives in the form of a new drug that he is given by a distant acquaintance.  It will allow him to access \\"100% of his brain.\\"  Suddenly he's got phenomenal cognitive abilities...particularly the ability to take random scraps of past things he's encountered and to put them together into patterns that no one else can recognize.  It's like the chess player who can envision every possible move and the outcomes of each of those moves.  Cooper finishes his book (and it's brilliant) and then sets about making some serious money with his new powers.    With such incredible powers, he seems to strive to accomplish relatively little.  Yes, he wants to make money...that is certainly understandable and relatable.  But he doesn't see a big pattern for himself.  And as a viewer, I was constantly distracted by thinking...\\"wow, if I had HIS abilities, I'd do so much more.\\"  It felt a little bit like a failure of the imagination on the part of the filmmakers.  Thus, I felt vaguely disappointed in the film...a great idea that boiled down to a somewhat predictable \\"corporate espionage\\" kind of thing.    Because Cooper discovers that this drug wasn't exactly developed with the approval of the FDA.  The side-effects, particularly of not taking the drug every day, are unpleasant.  And there are some unpleasant, even murderous folks who want the drug, or at least want the abilities that Cooper now has to work to their advantage.  Who can he trust?  How will he get away from all the folks who are now following him?  What will he do when his stock of pills runs out?    It's all interesting but a bit generic.  Cooper makes a pretty good leading man here, but he generates little chemistry with the bland Abbie Cornish...so we are not terribly invested in their relationship.  Robert DeNiro is on hand to collect a paycheck as a big shot executive trying to pull of the most ambitious corporate merger in history...and he needs the talent Cooper brings to the table.      The most amusing part of the movie is when Cooper has to borrow his initial stake in the stock market from a Russian loan shark, who inadvertently discovers the pill Cooper is taking.  Suddenly, this thuggish character becomes a LOT more dangerous to Cooper than the corporate big-shots.  This showed some creativity and the menace and humor of these scenes was welcome.    I recommend LIMITLESS, but my admiration is not...well...limitless.  Cooper is good.  The basic concept is intriguing.  The \\"action\\" shenanigans are fairly tense, but feel lifted from hundreds of movies that came before.  I realize this is a lazy thing to say, but I'd say wait until it comes out on DVD.

B004LSJCJM|5.0|Whenever Joe Bonamassa puts out a new album, I try to give it a lot of spins before I write a review.  He has yet to release a record that I don't enjoy, but I certainly like some more than others and it usually takes at least a week's worth of constant rotation before I can really judge where it falls in the pantheon of his work.  After receiving my pre-order of 'Dust Bowl' a full week before the release date and having it spinning non-stop since, I can safely say that this is easily Joe's best record since 'You and Me', and possibly right up there with the best he's ever done, alongside the aforementioned 'You and Me' and his early classic 'Blues Deluxe'.      Since 'You and Me', Joe has released a lot of great music, but this is the first truly great ALBUM.  'Sloe Gin' had a lot of knockout moments but lacked a lot of the fiery lead playing and didn't feel like a cohesive whole.  'The Ballad of John Henry' felt much more like a complete album, but it dragged in spots and for me, lacked a certain fire.  'Black Rock' blew me away at first with it's return to some of the blazing lead work, but the material was so all over the place that it didn't seem to have a natural flow to it.  'Dust Bowl' brings everything together.  Now, some old school fans are going to be disappointed that this isn't 'Blues Deluxe II'.  But you have to realize that Joe is no longer the artist that made that record.  He has moved beyond his rag-tag power trio knocking out rocked-up versions of blues chestnuts.  He's really become an artist, and he does a great job here of taking chances and keeping things varied, while never losing that signature Joe sound.  The originals and cover meld together perfectly, and the guest spots are brilliantly chosen.  Kevin Shirley's production is beefy, muscular, and spacious, so the album sounds loud and ballsy even when played at low volume.  Joe's vocals continue to get stronger and stronger with each passing release, and he really pours himself into his vocal performance here, and the lyrics to his original tracks are some of the strongest and most mature he's ever written.  And then of course, the guitar playing is simply off the charts brilliant.  The album kicks off with 'Slow Train', an insistent, stomping groover that kicks and snorts like a mule.  The tempo is slow, but the energy is high, so the song never sounds plodding the way some of the slower tracks on 'John Henry' did.  'Slow Train' then gives way to the title track, a deliciously slinky groover with great lyrics and a fantastic bass line.  Joe (and Kevin Shirley, I'm sure) wisely resisted the urge to have the tune explode into a power-chord orgy in the chorus, instead slowly building the intensity over the course of the entire song and allowing it to climax with Joe's soaring lead solo instead.  'Tennessee Plates' is up next, and is placed perfectly in the running order as a lighthearted yin to the powerful yang of the first 2 cuts.  It's a country boogie-blues cover of the Hiatt original, with Hiatt and Joe dueting on lead vocals and Vince Gill contributing some fantastic lead playing.  This is followed by 'The Meaning of the Blues', a slow-burn but intense blues featuring a great lead vocal from Joe.  Joe's leads are passionate and emotional, the kind that have you making 'guitar faces' while you're playing along on the air guitar.  Another Joe original, 'Black Lung Heartache' come next.  It starts off with some of the native Greek instrumentation that Joe first explored on 'Black Rock' before giving way to a grinding guitar orgy and, of course, some amazing leads.  This is one of the best original tracks that Joe has penned to date, simply great stuff.  No Joe album would be complete without the obligatory 12-bar shuffle blues, and we get that here in the form of the rollicking 'You Better Watch Yourself'.     The volume now gets turned down a notch for 'The Last Matador of Bayonne', a gorgeous and deliberate acoustic flavored number with a great,emotional lead vocal.  This track almost acts as an 'intermission' of sorts, but that's not to say it's a throwaway, as it's a beautifully constructed song and another of Joe's strongest ever original tunes.  It's followed by a cover of Free's 'Heartbreaker' featuring a vocal duet between Joe and his Black Country Communion bandmate, the 'Voice of Rock', Glenn Hughes.  I'm a huge fan of Free (and just about anything related to Paul Rodgers) and this cover is done with great reverence and emotion.  Joe's husky rasp and Glenn's soaring rock voice blend perfectly, and makes me hope there's be more shared lead vocals on the next BCC record!  'No Love on the Street' features some very nice strings and probably the best lead solo on the record.  From about 2:30 on you are bathed in an absolute downpour of incandescent, wah-wah drenched flurries that are simply awe-inspiring.  The tempo is then revved back up for 'The Whale that Swallowed Jonah', and rolling and tumbling Joe-penned rocker with a catchy chorus that has been one of my favorite tracks on the album.  Another duet is next, this time with Vince Gill (who previously contributed guitar work on 'Tennessee Plates') on his bouncy 'Sweet Rowena'.  Gill's smooth voice is another great foil for Joe's and they meld together perfectly, and Gill's country flavored picking is also a welcome addition.  The album winds up with 'Prisoner', a song originally performed by Barbra Streisand of all people.  Joe and co. have turned it into a soaring, minor-key blues rock ballad that would not sound out of place on a Free album circa 1972.     'Dust Bowl' is one of Joe Bonamassa's crowning achievements to date.  It's one of the most cohesive and well rounded albums he has ever recorded and will be hard to top the next time out.  Joe's albums are almost always a home run, but once in awhile he hits one of those steroid-assited, 600 foot Mark McGwire jobs, and 'Dust Bowl' is one of those.  This is a great place to start for new fans, and should delight all but the most stubborn of his old ones.

B004A8ZWSS|4.0|THE TOURIST has many flaws, not the least of which is the fact that it doesn't seem to be able to make up its mind whether it is a suspense thriller, a romantic comedy, or a travelogue. B001OQCV6K|3.0|When this movie was announced, I was thrilled to bits and very excited. As a big fan of Sherlock Holmes, here was a legitimate big screen adaptation of the brilliant works of Arthur Conan Doyle. December arrived and the movie opened very well. By the time I got to seeing it (on BluRay)it was a big hit worldwide. 0439023521|5.0|In the ruins of North America, a country called Panem has arisen from the ashes. Consisting of a Capitol and twelve Districts, the country is strict with its citizens. To commemorate the victory of the Capitol over the districts that revolted against it early in the nation's history, each year Panem has an event called the Hunger Games. Twenty-four competitors - one boy and one girl from each District - are selected for the honor of representing their home in an arena where only one will emerge victorious. For the winner, a victory means fame, fortune, and an easier life.    However, the Hunger Games are a far cry from the Olympics. Part Roman gladiators, part Survivor, each contestant - or \\"tribute - is armed with one set of clothes and sent into a strange land. The rules are simple: the last one alive wins. Tributes are encouraged to kill each other, outsmart each other in terms of food and water supplies, and must rely on their wits and the elements to battle disease, fire, flood, and any other surprises that might arise.     Katniss Everdeen is sixteen the year her 12-year old sister, Prim, is selected to represent District 12 in the games. She quickly volunteers to take Prim's place, and is quickly whisked away to a world she's never seen in the Capitol to prepare for what is literally a battle for her life. Her co-tribute from District 12 is Peeta Mellark, a boy she knows from school who saved her from starvation several years earlier. As the two prepare for the strange competition (which is shown on live television across the country), they form a strange bond. But how much is for the cameras - companies sponsor tributes they are supporting - and how much is reality? And will it matter when they must kill each other to survive?    Suzanne Collins' \\"The Hunger Games\\" is a frightening novel about a post-apocalyptic community where this type of horror is seen as spectacle, where the perversity of having children fight each other to the death is a sport to be waged upon, and where human life and love have no real value. Early on, I found myself disturbed by the premise, but I was completely absorbed in the story. This isn't one of those so-bad-you-can't-look-away trainwrecks of a book. It's a well-written, realistic take on a world that we hopefully will never encounter. And while I don't usually go for dystopian tales, I couldn't put it down.

B004H4XI5O|4.0|Joshua Foer is a writer for Slate. Back in 2005 he covered an obscure event for memory enthusiasts. From there he delved deeper into what memory is, how it works, what happens when it doesn't, as well as what happens when it works too well. In the process, he prepared for and a year later actually won the 2006 U.S. Memory championships, and went on to compete at the international level.     Foer writes in an engaging manner, and structures his book well. Moonwalking had my interest from the beginning, and held it all the way to the end: I finished it in four days, despite a hellish schedule.     Inside you'll find an interview with the real life Rain Man, the one Dustin Hoffman's character was based on. You'll meet a man who could remember 1950, but nothing after that, including that you had a conversation with him five minutes ago.     Joshua also describes in great detail the tricks he used and the regimen he stuck by to compete (and win) in the U.S. Memory Championship of 2006. You'll also learn the history behind the use of memory in ancient times, up until the Renaissance, why it works the way it does, and what our memories aren't so good at.     I recommend this book to anyone even remotely curious about how the mind works, and why they forget where they put their car keys.     For deeper (and thus drier) study, please see The Art of Memory, by Frances Yates.     Trivia: the author used an image of himself moonwalking with Einstein to remember the combination of cards I used as the title for this review.

I've listened to the original Iron Man, oh, I don't know, maybe ten thousand times, and I could listen to it ten thousand more with no trace of boredom or indifference.  Besides the other historic songs like War Pigs, the song Paranoid, and Fairies Wear Boots, this album offers plenty more great music.  It has Planet Caravan, a mellow, moving, trippy psychedelic excursion.  Electric Funeral, the ultra-heavy precursor to both doom metal and speed metal.  Hand of Doom, featuring Iommi's dynamic songwriting and the startling contrast of cool, jazzy verses giving way to thundering heavy metal.  And finally, the unforgettable Rat Salad, which is my favorite Sabbath instrumental piece.  Not much reason not to love Paranoid.<br /><br />I don't want to leave out the unique, distinct, buzz that is Geezer Butler's bass sound, and Bill Ward's versatile, unconventional drum patterns.  Add these elements to Ozzy's first truly confident vocal performance and you have one of the greatest releases in music history.  Definitely no sophomore slump here for the budding superstars and future legends.

0671835467|5.0|I adore Anne Frank: The Diary of a Young Girl and have read it multiple times over the years. Anne's diary has come to mean different things to me as I've grown older - when I first read it as a ten-year-old girl, I was fascinated by the hidden life she led with her family and there was a sense of dread and excitement about her precarious circumstances, the danger of being discovered. When I read Anne's diary again as a teenager, I came to empathize with Anne's frustrations with her mother and the other adults around her whom Anne felt did not understand her and treated her as a child. I felt Anne's desire to be 'heard' and not just treated as an inconvenience or child was so much aligned with what I was going through during adolescence.<br /><br />When I re-read the diary as an adult, I came to appreciate all the other things - the tragedy of having lost such an amazing and talented young voice in the most horrible of circumstances; the beauty of Anne's writing which is all the more amazing given how young she was when she wrote this diary; and the themes of alienation, fear, and hope. Now, as the mother of a young daughter, I read this diary again and gain another fresh perspective - of the complex relationship between daughter and mother. Anne had a strained relationship with her mother Edith, and clearly gravitated toward her beloved Pim (father). This was obvious even before Anne and her family were compelled to move into hiding in the Annex. As a mother, reading Anne's thoughts about her mother (often unflattering) was a disconcerting experience, though things do improve somewhat later on.<br /><br />Anne Frank's Diary of a Young Girl is a classic not only of Holocaust literature, but also a classic Young Adult read with its myriad themes that will appeal to both adolescents and adult readers.

1400052173|4.0|Read this book not for the strong writing (it's readable writing but not great writing) nor for the lovable, upbeat characters (this is a story of a family in constant struggle.)  Read this book for a great narrative about medicine, race, research and ethics.    This is the second book I have read about research on human cells, the first being Michael Crichton's NEXT.  I don't know the right answer, but these books are raising the right questions.      Henrietta Lacks was a poor black cancer patient at Johns Hopkins in the fifties.  Cell culture was in its infancy when samples of her tumor were removed for study.  Her cells, for reasons barely understood even today, survived in the lab and grew quickly, making them perfect for many different types of cell research.    Henrietta's cells, named HeLa, are famous, but her family never knew until late in the game.  This book is about their attempts to educate themselves and to understand what had happened to their mother and their family.    The author also introduces us to a wide range of medical experimentation that was done on black patients over the years, which provides a context for why Henrietta's family might not embrace medical research in a positive way.    It's a compelling story, thoughtfully and carefully told.

B0010SKUYM|5.0|With 893 reviews already written, one might imagine that everything that could possibly be said about this book (Patrick Rothfuss's \\"The Name of the Wind) has been said and nothing more could be said.  The fact that so many people felt compelled to write a review, including myself, also tells you that this book has had a substantial impact on almost everybody who read it.  Many people have already commented on what a great story-teller Rothfuss is.  Fewer commented on the compelling world of magic, poverty, and music presented in the book.  I will focus on these three subjects.      In Rothfuss' world, magic obey laws that are similar to those that govern thermodynamics.  The first law states that energy is conserved.  Indeed, energy must come from somewhere and Rothfuss took great pains to illustrate this.  It was of of course convenient that the story was set at a University where professors teach young people the principles of magic.  The concept of \\"sympathy\\" and attaching objects to each other is not just interesting but ingenious.  Likewise, the attempt to explain the importance of naming of the wind, the title of the book, was remarkably well done.        Probably the most consistent and best-described situation in the First book was the poverty of the protagonist.  The description of the hunger, the desperation, and the fear that extreme poverty brings is so real that it seems likely the author himself must have been poor at one point.  The crueity of people towards each other, particularly the destitute, was described in devastating detail.  He truly understands how much a little bit of money is worth when one is hungry or really wants something that only money can buy.  Yet, at the same time, Ruthfuss understands that money isn't everything.    Finally, I imagine that Rothfuss is an accomplished lute player, or at least a guitar player.  Nobody who is not a player can understand or describe so vividly what it means to perform with closed eyes and the feelings that it evokes in the player.  The view was not from the outside but from the inside of the player.  He understands the hunger for the instrument, the feeling of having lost of damaged it.  In almost every scene, music was part of the book and the emotional makeup of the book.      At first I was puzzled by why some people felt so strongly negative about the book.  Most of the reactions were not boredom but strong dislike.  It seems that the book aroused strong feelings in readers.  Over 80% liked it very much and many said that it was amongst the best books that they have ever read.  But, 5-10% felt very strongly that the book was terrible.  It is hard to imagine why except that the book seemed to rub these people the wrong way.  But, after having finished reading the book, it became clear that it may be the character.  You either loved or strongly disliked the main character.      Wise.

0451169514|5.0|Let me begin by saying that I find almost all Stephen King books very entertaining and readable. Believability is a different matter, however.<br /><br />I tend to divide Stephen King books into the \\"Supernatural\\" and the \\"Non-Supernatural.\\" As for the Non-Supernatural, my favorites are Misery and On Writing, and the short story/novella Rita Hayworth and the Shawshank Redemption. Stephen King's Supernatural books, the ones that contain some supernatural element central to the plot, generally leave me wanting more. Too often for me the supernatural comes in as a \\"deus ex machina\\" (Latin for \\"God out of the machine,\\" a plot device where a very difficult problem is suddenly solved with the contrived intervention of some new event, ability, or supernatural phenomenon). Although The Stand is a good read, the end of that book is a good example of deus ex machina. Not so with It.<br /><br />Stephen King's It is completely engrossing, genuinely scary, and very endearing believe it or not. The central characters are a group of self-proclaimed \\"losers\\" who don't fit in with the other kids in school. They naturally gravitate towards one another and become a very tight group of friends in the small town of Derry Maine during a seemingly everlasting summer in the 1950's. They make the swampy bottom area of town called the Barrens their personal clubhouse, and each of their unique talents and family backgrounds come into play.<br /><br />Derry is cursed by a being they call \\"It,\\" which has inhabited the town for hundreds of years. It appears to each person as the sum of their own worst fears, forcing them to face their own demons in turn. The story goes back and forth between the group in the 1950's as children and the group in present day as grownups, gathered back in Derry to face It again after it resurfaces 28 years later and resumes abducting and killing children.<br /><br />The genius of Stephen King in It is how he sees things most of us take for granted - the drains in our bathroom, the sewer system, abandoned buildings in town, etc. - and turns them into a vast underworld where evil exists right under our very noses without us knowing a thing about it until it is (almost?) too late.<br /><br />The chemistry of the central characters of the book as children and then again as adults is flawless. They reminded me of the characters in Stephen King's Stand By Me (the movie version of his terrific novella The Body from his collection Different Seasons), only these characters are much more fleshed out and are even more engrossing. You will find yourself desperately wanting to be a member of the gang and hanging out with them in the Barrens all summer long.<br /><br />Stephen King's It is absolutely terrific. Do not be put off by its length - trust Stephen King as a master storyteller here. Pick it up and read 1-2 chapters a night, and it will sweep you along faster and faster like a mounting hurricane. By the end you will be yearning for more. Highly recommended.bandoned buildings in town, etc. - and turns them into a vast underworld where evil exists right under our very noses without us knowing a thing about it until it is (almost?) too late. 1936594366|4.0|Siddhartha is a philosophical novel about one man's quest for spiritual enlightenment. The story takes place about 500 BC in India. Siddhartha, the son of a Hindu Brahmin, groomed to become a Brahmin himself, turns his back on the teachings of his father and leaves his village to search for inner peace. He renounces all worldly possessions and joins the samanas, ascetic mendicants who live in the forest. Siddhartha then meets Gotama, the Buddha, but ultimately decides to seek his own path rather than follow the Buddha's teachings. At this point Siddhartha turns his back on his thoughtful life of fasting and meditation, and begins a worldly life of lust and greed. Though the meaning of life continues to elude Siddhartha, and he finds each new lifestyle to be inadequate to answer his metaphysical questions, along the way he learns something valuable from each stage of his journey, and this accumulation of knowledge and experience advances him step by step on the path towards enlightenment.<br /><br />Hermann Hesse is a masterful writer who deservedly won the Nobel Prize in 1946. Many of his novels revolve around the quest for knowledge and wisdom, yet all differ greatly in their setting and tone. Siddhartha is written in deceptively simple prose. It reads almost like a fairy tale, yet Hesse's economy of words masks a rich depth of philosophical insight and spiritual understanding. It is a very short novel, easy to read, and accessible to readers of all levels from junior high to PhD, though the deeper philosphical concepts may escape younger readers. Due to its brevity and the inherent ambiguity in its spiritual subject matter, this novel can be enjoyed again and again, with new discoveries made in each rereading. It provides a good introduction to the philosphy of eastern religions, Hinduism and Buddhism in particular, though one may also recognize in the text elements from schools of western philosophical thought like pantheism, stoicism, and cynicism (in the Greek sense of the word). But a big part of the message of the novel is that enlightenment cannot be reached by labeling movements or perusing texts. One can only find inner peace by living life, by experiencing. Wisdom can be learned but cannot be taught. For those engaged in their own quest, this book does not provide a treasure map with a big red X where lie all the answers to your metaphysical queries. It's more like one solitary signpost along a winding path, with a simple arrow pointing the way.sense of the word). But a big part of the message of the novel is that enlightenment cannot be reached by labeling movements or perusing texts. One can only find inner peace by living life, by experiencing. B0021L8V0W|4.0|As I've said several times before, the sub-genre of spy films (or espionage/spy thrillers as they're sometimes referred) has long been a popular well for Hollywood to draw from for inspiration and box office success. Typically these films have centered on a tough-as-nails male action hero type, many times with a strong desire for women and specific types of alcoholic beverages. In 2010, that all changed with the release of the action-packed thrill ride \\"Salt\\" starring Angelina Jolie (\\"Wanted\\") in the title role.    \\"Salt\\" chronicles the espionage powder keg that explodes when CIA agent Evelyn Salt (Angelina Jolie) is accused of being a double agent for Russia tasked with assassinating the President of the United States. With her loyalty in question, her career in jeopardy, and the life she's known being turned on its ear; Salt will do whatever it takes to clear her name, reclaim her life, and prevent an international incident from occurring before time runs out.    Let me start by saying this: leave it to Angelina Jolie to be the first woman to successfully infiltrate this predominantly male headlined sub-genre of film. Not only that, but with her acting prowess, determination and physicality, be able to convincingly portray a character that can stand toe-to-toe with Bond and Bourne. Of course, this shouldn't be that surprising given that Angelina has proven time and again through various roles that there is not a single genre in which she cannot find success.    As the character of Evelyn Salt, Angelina is truly run through a veritable gauntlet of physical and emotional tests. In one scene she can be scaling the side of a very tall building (a stunt she performed herself) or witnessing a horrible act as she tries to remain stoic, but a subtly quivering lip belies her inner turmoil. Or desperately trying to convince those around her of the truth; no matter what the scene calls for Angelina always delivers to perfection.    I don't think there are too many other actresses out there, if any, that could have believably pulled off such a demanding role that is rife with intensity, duplicity, drama, and action. Sure several actresses could have handled some of the facets of the character, but all of them? In this I believe Angelina stands alone, and that is why I believe her to be probably the best, and certainly the most versatile, actress of her generation.    An interesting side note for the character is that originally Tom Cruise (\\"Mission: Impossible\\") was attached to the role, but reportedly dropped out to film the romantic action comedy \\"Knight and Day\\". Thus, prompting the role to be rewritten from a male to a female, supposedly with Angelina in mind from then on.    Anyway, alongside Angelina are two very talented actors in the primary supporting roles, Liev Schreiber (\\"X-Men Origins: Wolverine\\") and Chiwetel Ejiofor (\\"Four Brothers\\").    Liev plays a fellow CIA agent of Salt's named Ted Winter, and is the only person at the agency who seems to believe that she is innocent of the accusations against her. Liev's character is a mixture of the typical no-nonsense agent, but with a healthy sense of humor and a protective, brotherly relationship with Salt. Aside from those character traits, Liev also imbues the character with charm and an underlying sense that he will do whatever it takes to protect Salt whether she's innocent or guilty.    All together Liev's performance makes his character much more memorable than one would expect from such a role. Plus, the early scenes between him and Salt bantering back and forth are enjoyable to watch.    As for Chiwetel Ejiofor, he plays a government operative named Peabody who unlike Winter is not inclined to simply believe in Salt's innocence, despite her years of service to the red, white, and blue. For the most part, Ejiofor plays the role by the book as the clichd government stooge that is blind to anything except the evidence on display. Even with this fairly one-dimensional character, Chiwetel still manages to make his scenes pop, thanks entirely to his charismatic disposition.    One more bit of casting I would like to discuss before moving on, and this one in particular struck me as odd when watching this film. Why, oh why did Andre Braugher (\\"Fantastic 4: Rise of the Silver Surfer\\") sign on to the most thankless role of Secretary of Defense? With an actor of his caliber and credibility, one would expect that he signed on for the role due to it being such a good character. However, he's given nothing to do other than run around in one scene and hand phones to people, all the while saying only a sentence of dialogue.    Seriously, this is a role that should have been handled by some no-name actor or even someone like Cuba Gooding, Jr. who has been relegated to the realm of Direct-to-DVD. Wasting the talent of Andre Braugher on a role such as this is completely mind-boggling and somewhat insulting to his long-lasting career. But, overall this was a minor issue and I digress.    Of course the cast would be nothing without the script written by Kurt Wimmer (\\"A Perfect Getaway\\"), who has proven yet again to be a terrific storyteller. Loaded with twists and turns, gripping action set pieces, and engaging drama, the story for \\"Salt\\" is entertaining from start to finish.    Understandably with a movie like this one has to be careful in how much is revealed, because the risk of spoiling is incredibly high. So, the only other thing I will say about this film's story is this: at the end of the movie, I guarantee you will be left begging for more.    Now I would be remiss if I didn't also mention the other major contributor to this movie's success, director Phillip Noyce (\\"Clear and Present Danger\\"). With a movie so jam-packed with fast-paced, hard-hitting action scenes one would expect the movie to have been directed by someone more oriented with the action genre. Not to say that Phillip Noyce is unfamiliar with action scenes, it's just his films aren't generally so heavy in this area. Even so, he directs the pulse-pounding action with as sure a hand as he does during the quieter, more dramatic moments.    Also, Noyce's excellent directing and pacing of the film ensured that the story was provided plenty of breathing room to unfold naturally; that way it wouldn't seem as if the story was there to serve the action rather than the other way around. And the use of flashbacks, which can backfire for some films, was perfect for providing character development and context, while breaking up the monotony of linear storytelling. It's no wonder why Phillip Noyce is heralded as a master of the espionage thriller.    All in all, \\"Salt\\" is a thoroughly entertaining movie, filled to the brim with excitement and intrigue. This film and its star, Angelina Jolie, prove that they belong in the pantheon of spy thrillers and hopefully we haven't seen the last of Evelyn Salt.    \\"Salt\\" is rated PG-13 for violence and language.

B002SJIO4A|4.0|This is a rough ride.  Not exactly a knee-slapper, no.  That's probably because this is astonishingly close to being accurate.  If aliens did arrive and we had any sort of advantage over them whatsoever, they would be exploited and subjugated by us.  This is science fiction that works.  It taps into the cultural zeitgeist and forces us to look at ourselves via a \\"what if\\" scenario.  The analogy to apartheid should be clear to anyone, with the aliens as stand-ins for the black population that is \\"ready to take up arms against their oppressors\\" to paraphrase Bono.  Humans even manufacture a derogatory term for the aliens (as we are so good at doing): \\"Prawns.\\"  And there is debate within the film as to whether or not it is proper to call them \\"Prawns,\\" with one government official defending the slur by saying, \\"Well let's face it, it's what they look like, isn't it?\\"  Indeed they are rather Lovecraftian in appearance.  It doesn't stop there.  The \\"Prawns\\" are reduced to eating cat food (even though they like it), there are horrendous medical experiments on them that amount to little more than torture (shades of Mengele), and an overall statement of \\"man's inhumanity to other living things.\\"  Usually in the name of a profit.  The film is left open to a sequel.  Should that come to pass, I would hope that the filmmakers include a bit of \\"prequel\\" as well, explaining who the Prawns are and how they came to us in such a disheveled state.  Make no mistake, this is a good film and quite worthy of its Best Picture nomination last year.  But don't ever expect it to be rollicking good, 'splodey sci-fi or some such drivel.  You'll be lucky to make it through without shedding a tear or turning away in revulsion at times.

B004GHYCKW|5.0|English singer/songwriter PJ Harvey has released a number of great records since her classic debut, often changing her style and - aside from maybe Uh Huh Her - making sure that each record has its own identity. For her eighth proper studio record, the great Let England Shake, Harvey is changing things up more than ever, offering something of a new vocal style, a new focal instrument (the autoharp), some strange ornamentation (saxophones, you name it), and a very heavy and constant focus (war). All that said, this still somehow feels very much like a PJ Harvey record, offering all the bite and howl of her best work while giving the listener plenty to think about.    Long known not only for her howl, but also for her songwriting ability (see White Chalk or To Bring You My Love for proof), Harvey seems maybe more focused here than ever, admitting in the media that she spent quite a bit of time studying the history of conflict and war while writing her new record. And while its easy to listen to these 12 songs and think about things going on in the world today, most of the themes and concepts can be traced back through time, Harvey often referencing Anzac Cove, not Afghanistan. And while I do find the lyrics to be particularly interesting and even rewarding (Harvey supposedly spent over two years simply perfecting the lyrics with not instruments, but pen and paper), what I find most rewarding about this record are Harvey's arrangements and singing, which are as good here as ever.    It's not that I don't care about war or respect someone writing so elegantly about the evil of men (primarily in Europe, though I imagine it's hard for an American to hear the record and not feel like she's talking Bush and Obama), it's just that, well, I like music far more than I like war. War bums me out; music shoots straight to my mental state of well being in a way that nothing else can. The mixture of the two things, when you think about it, is strange; but, when done properly, it can work quite well. And while Harvey is often singing about sad history, you'd only know it if you paid close attention to her words with each spin - something I don't recommend. There's too much beauty in these often strange compositions to overlook. My theory is that, since Harvey, such a skilled album-maker at this point, took so long to pen these tunes, she very likely also had a whole lot of time to think about how she wanted to approach them in the studio. And so she came in with new voice, new sound and focused words, ready to record some of her most realized work ever.    Back when I was working in record stores I'd play Is This Desire? and, more than almost anything, Stories From the City, Stories From the Sea very, very often. Both albums had songs that appealed to me instantly, not unlike Let England Shake tracks such as \\"The Last Living Rose,\\" \\"England\\" and \\"The Words That Maketh Murder.\\" But, as I stuck with those two records, they revealed themselves to me in new ways, growing as I listened, eventually becoming front-to-back favorites. England is no different, once again proving Harvey as a true blue AOR-styled artist with some serious longevity.    Take an artist, any artist, with loads of great albums. Picture that catalog and all its high points. Now imagine me telling you that that very artist has yet to release their best record. Chances are, you wouldn't believe me. I wouldn't believe me. But here we have PJ Harvey who, after two decades of releasing great - even classic, in some cases - records, has released what could very likely go down as her signature record. It's the music. It's the singing. It's the writing. It's the strange ornamentation. Everything comes together here to form a solid, memorable work that ups the ante for anyone trying to make a serious album made to move and inspire. That the backdrops are also so unique and cool is one hell of a bonus.    Check out more of my reviews at [...]

B001P06GX4|5.0|From reading the description of this game online I was a little confused what they meant by only allowing pieces to touch at the corners because it wasn't readily apparent to me that most of the pieces consist of multiple joined squares in various configurations (think tetrus). Having said that, once you see the game the rules are quite simple and can be learned in a few minutes. Adults can play a challenging game, but 2 adults can readily play with 2 kids because the adults will focus on blocking each other in and hence the [ignored] kids could well end up winning. Best with 4 players, but 2 works pretty well too.      Caveats: the playing pieces are bit flimsy, so kids who aren't careful could break some pieces, but replacement pieces are easily (and cheaply) available. Also, the scoring rules are a bit more complex than the playing rules, so when we play with kids we just say whoever is the last one able to make a move is the winner (and 2nd last one gets 2nd place, etc).

The Strokes were trapped in a box.    So here we are, 5 years later, with an album that even the band themselves seem unsure of.  Check out current interviews with Julian and Nick and you'll find quotes along the lines of \\"yeah I like it, but there's a lot of things I wouldn't have done.\\" and \\"i feel we have a better album in us.\\"  The first quote, by Julian, is reflecting on the fact that this time around, all five members had equal say in calling the shots and writing the songs. And it shows in both a good and bad way.    The bad, is that the other members of this band are simply not as talented songwriters as Julian.  99% of the material by this band has been written by Julian. Fans may love the entire band, but its Julian's songwriting that is the foundation for it all. I'll admit that certain tracks on here written by the others are better than I'd expect, but none of them are going to hit you like \\"12:51\\" or \\"Last Nite\\" did. There are also a few songs I don't think should have made the record.    The good, is that it seems evident that Julian's band members pushed to try out some new sounds.  Despite the fact that the lead single sounds like classic Strokes, theres a lot of stuff on here that really feels like new territory. I was so surprised that the production style on \\"Two Kinds of Happiness\\" actually worked for the band. It made me really happy, because even though the song isn't a 10/10, I felt like I finally heard The Strokes coming out of their little box while still sounding GOOD.    This review might sound a little harsh, but I think it has to be.  With all five members writing and putting their ideas on the table, this is in every way a brand newStrokes, a Strokes that even the band themselves aren't quite sure of just yet.  Some ideas really worked well, some of them fell a bit flat. Regardless, it's very good to finally hear from this band again.  I feel they have some great albums ahead of them, and this is the first step in figuring out how to make them a reality.

You have been warned:<br /><br />Although I have never read Picoult's work and always viewed her a fluff fiction writer, I am glad that I gave this book a chance, because she bravely confronts such a deeply controversial issue. Her greatest strengths in this novel lie in the characters of Zoe and Vanessa. Both women are deeply sympathetic, but they have their flaws, too. Piccoult never depicts these women and their allies as saints.<br /><br />With that said, the remaining cast show up poorly against these two strong characters. Max, for example, is very shallow. All he does is parrot his pastor and allow himself to be emotionally manipulated by his friends. Although I did sympathize with him at first when Zoe seemed to place childbearing above their marriage, he became increasingly difficult to relate to later on as he lost all semblance of independent thought. To be fair, I experienced the same level of disconnection with Zoe's lawyer, Angela Moretti. At first, I liked her sense of humor and felt that she would make a great advocate for Zoe, but was disappointed to see her take cheap shots at Max and his lawyer, Wade Preston. There were many instances in which she applied sound logic to refute Preston's arguments, and I believe she should have stayed on that high road instead of resorting to school yard taunts.<br /><br />Finally, although the surprise ending was beautiful and appropriate, the epilogue left more questions than it answered. For instance, Picoult implies that Max and his brother's wife, Liddy, eventually got married. I am wondering how they accomplished this since Liddy and Max's brother Reid are conservative Christians who probably wouldn't believe in divorce.. I can't imagine that Reid would let his wife go without a fight, nor can I imagine Max sabotaging his relationship with the brother that he loved. Piccoult should have explored the outcome of these relationships more thoroughly.<br /><br />Despite its flaws in plot and character, I still enjoyed this book. 0756404738|3.0|I enjoyed this book.  I did not, however, enjoy it as much as I enjoyed NotW.  It just wasn't as good, I felt; which is to say that [for me] it was still better than most of what one finds in the fantasy genre these days.    While not as tight of a telling as Day One was, I can still see purpose in Day Two.  There's possibly good explanation for the wandering, sometimes aimless feel of Day Two.  Current-day Kote is retelling this story.  Day One contained a lot of foundational detail that Kote recalled quite vividly, maybe because it all occurred in his most formative years.  Day Two content is drawn from when Kvothe was in the throes of teenage turbulence and distractability.  Is it any wonder, then, that the story is not quite as well-constructed in the retelling?  That it meanders?  That it over-focuses on some things while glossing over others?  Viewed in that light, it makes sense to me.    That said, there were still parts that could have been pared down without discernable loss to the overall story and at great benefit to the pacing.      Still, the story is Kote's retelling of his life as Kvothe.  Thus, he's telling the details that stand out the most *to him*.  Even the aspects of Day Two that we can't currently see have any value to the story may reappear in Day Three, causing us to suddenly go, \\"Ohhhhhh! So *that's* why all that stuff about the Adem mattered in Day Two of the telling!\\"    Overall, I found the story mostly engaging.  Some of the word choices of the author served to yank me out of the story at times.  Certainly, the pacing didn't meet my expectations based on the pacing of Day One.  And some parts went beyond the bounds of dull into the territory of trite and repetitive and occasionally what I considered as sub-quality writing compared to what I know Pat to be capable of. ***[SLIGHT SPOILER]***  Felurian was naked.  We get it.  No need to remind us a half-dozen times per page for 50 pages.  Even if I could excuse the constant hyper-focus ofKvothe on the naked physique of a female, Kote [who, let's not forget, is retelling the story] should be well past that sort of hyper-focus and, I daresay, somewhat jaded to it by now.       One of the best qualities I found in NotW was that Pat didn't talk down to his readers but seemed to expect us to piece some things together on our own without him spelling everything out for us.  Parts of WMF seemed to violate that, which disappointed me.    So while better than many fantasy novels, WMF didn't quite measure up to the balance and wholeness I sensed in NotW.  Like anything in life, it has its good and bad points.  The bad points were not bad enough to put me off the story.  The good points were good enough to keep me coming back.
